the talk is simple and that is most people think of an agent as the model or the execution environment that it's running in and I think that that's the

wrong abstraction. I think that the thing that actually gives an agent its identity is its log. And that's what I'm

going to be arguing today. So, think about a character you've spent a 100 hours playing in your favorite video
6:47:51

6:48:01
6 hours, 48 minutes, 1 second
No, it's not. Those things matter and those things are what we'll interact with and they'll run the character. But none of those things are your character.
6:48:09
6 hours, 48 minutes, 9 seconds
Your character is data. It's the save file. And this is important because if your PlayStation bursts into flames,

your character isn't gone. You can buy another PlayStation. You can download your save file from the cloud and you

6:48:32
6 hours, 48 minutes, 32 seconds
all captured in its data. The character lives in the data. And this is the framing that I want to bring to agents.
6:48:41
6 hours, 48 minutes, 41 seconds
Today when people talk about agents, they usually point at the wrong thing.
6:48:46
6 hours, 48 minutes, 46 seconds
They'll say that the agent is the model or they'll say that it's the runtime.
6:48:49
6 hours, 48 minutes, 49 seconds
And again, as I mentioned earlier, those things matter, but they're not the agent. The agent is its data. It's
6:48:56
6 hours, 48 minutes, 56 seconds
specifically the log. So what actually is the log? At the simplest level, the log is the appendon event history of the
6:49:05
6 hours, 49 minutes, 5 seconds
agent. It's every user input, every model output, every tool call, tool result, permission, failure. And the
6:49:13
6 hours, 49 minutes, 13 seconds
idea is that every state transition that the agent takes is written to the log.
6:49:19
6 hours, 49 minutes, 19 seconds
This is important because it means that the identity of the agent isn't tied to the runtime or the model or the tools.
6:49:28
6 hours, 49 minutes, 28 seconds
Those things are all just interpreting and appending to the log. They're reading the log, acting on it, and writing the next event back. And that's
6:49:36
6 hours, 49 minutes, 36 seconds
important because then just using the log on its own is enough to resume the
6:49:43
6 hours, 49 minutes, 43 seconds
agent. Once you define the agent as the log, the rest of the system becomes a whole lot easier to reason about because every operation is either reading from
6:49:52
6 hours, 49 minutes, 52 seconds
or appending to the log. The model is reading from the log and then determining the next action. The tool
6:50:00
6 hours, 50 minutes
runner is then executing that action and then it's appending that result. And this is all operating in a loop.
6:50:08
6 hours, 50 minutes, 8 seconds
Everything coordinates itself around the log. In practice, a simplified loop can look something like this. You can reconstruct the state from the log. You
6:50:16
6 hours, 50 minutes, 16 seconds
can pass that state to the model. The model can propose the next step and then append that response to the log. If the response asks for a tool, you can run
6:50:24
6 hours, 50 minutes, 24 seconds
that tool and also append that response to the log and then you can repeat. The important insight is not that this loop is complicated. The important insight is
6:50:33
6 hours, 50 minutes, 33 seconds
that the loop is disposable. A worker can claim the session, read the log, advance the agent one step, write the result, and then just completely
6:50:40
6 hours, 50 minutes, 40 seconds
disappear. And then that means that any other worker can pick it up later. This pattern should feel familiar.
6:50:48
6 hours, 50 minutes, 48 seconds
Databases had to learn this first. For years, databases looked like these non-transparent systems that were hard to reason about with tables and indexes
6:50:57
6 hours, 50 minutes, 57 seconds
and materialized views. Underneath every serious database is a log and that log is the durable sequence of changes.
6:51:08
6 hours, 51 minutes, 8 seconds
Everything else is a view. I think agents need the same inversion. Today agents are treated as again these
6:51:16
6 hours, 51 minutes, 16 seconds
complicated systems that are opaque and they're filled with models and prompts and tool calls. But for the durable
6:51:23
6 hours, 51 minutes, 23 seconds
session, the log should be primary. The context that gets fed into the model is a projection of that log. The UI that
6:51:30
6 hours, 51 minutes, 30 seconds
gets rendered on top is a projection of that log. Debugging and traceability is a projection. Auditing is a projection.
6:51:38
6 hours, 51 minutes, 38 seconds
Compaction is also a projection which we'll talk about. But the log itself is not a projection. The log is the durable history that all of these projections
6:51:47
6 hours, 51 minutes, 47 seconds
can come from. Now there are two objections to the log as the agent that are worth discussing. So I'm going to talk about them now. Now let's start
6:51:54
6 hours, 51 minutes, 54 seconds
with compaction. A law can grow indefinitely, but a model's view of it can't. Context windows are finite. So
6:52:03
6 hours, 52 minutes, 3 seconds
eventually you do need to compact the log into a smaller representation that the model can reason about. But the important point is that this compaction
6:52:11
6 hours, 52 minutes, 11 seconds
is not magic and it doesn't break the claim that the log is the agent.
6:52:16
6 hours, 52 minutes, 16 seconds
Compaction is lossy. A compacted summary is not going to perfectly reproduce the state of the agent in a smaller form.
6:52:24
6 hours, 52 minutes, 24 seconds
It's actually going to throw information away. Point is the full log is the record and a compaction is just one projection of it. Just like how a
6:52:33
6 hours, 52 minutes, 33 seconds
materialized view is not the database or a summary of a conversation is not the conversation. If you keep the raw log, you can always generate new projections
6:52:42
6 hours, 52 minutes, 42 seconds
from it. But if you throw away the law log and keep only the compaction, you've effectively lost part of the agent. So
6:52:49
6 hours, 52 minutes, 49 seconds
it's cleanest to treat compaction as a best effort lossy fork, one that you can resume as a new log. The second
6:52:58
6 hours, 52 minutes, 58 seconds
objection is what about tools that change state outside of the log? And that's true. An agent can edit a file.
6:53:05
6 hours, 53 minutes, 5 seconds
It can create a GitHub issue. It can send an email. So clearly there is state outside of the log. But the point is is that the log is not supposed to contain
6:53:14
6 hours, 53 minutes, 14 seconds
the whole world. The log is just the agent's view of the world. It's just like how in the video game in Skyrim, the Skyrim save file doesn't contain the
6:53:23
6 hours, 53 minutes, 23 seconds
entire game engine or every asset in the map. It just contains the player specific state which is needed to drop you back into that world. And the same
6:53:31
6 hours, 53 minutes, 31 seconds
is true for the log and agents. The log can only faithfully resume or store that agent's identity and its view of the
6:53:40
6 hours, 53 minutes, 40 seconds
world, but it cannot make that world deterministic. If the agent sent an email, forking back won't unend it. If
6:53:47
6 hours, 53 minutes, 47 seconds
some file got changed underneath, the agent won't know about it. But the log's job is to record what the agent did,
6:53:55
6 hours, 53 minutes, 55 seconds
what it saw, what changed, and what it needs to continue. It stores that identity, and that's its purpose. And
6:54:02
6 hours, 54 minutes, 2 seconds
much like that Skyrim character save file, it's not meant to store the entire world. It's just meant to store its view
6:54:10
6 hours, 54 minutes, 10 seconds
of it. So once you start treating the log as a primitive, a whole bunch of system properties will fall out naturally. So the first property is
6:54:18
6 hours, 54 minutes, 18 seconds
reliability. Consider what happens today with cloud code. If you're using cloud code and your agent reaches a permission
6:54:25

prompt and the process dies for whatever reason and then you resume it, the permission prompt will be gone and the
6:54:33
6 hours, 54 minutes, 33 seconds
agent will be paused and that is unacceptable in production. The permission prompt should stay there. So
6:54:41
6 hours, 54 minutes, 41 seconds
this is just a sign of when you architect your agent in a way where the log isn't the agent. when the log is the agent.
